<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Fluorine</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Fluorene" title="Fluorene">fluorene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 9</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Fluorine,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">9</span></sub>F</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg" title="Small sample of pale yellow liquid fluorine condensed in liquid nitrogen"><img alt="Small sample of pale yellow liquid fluorine condensed in liquid nitrogen" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="373" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg/220px-Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg/330px-Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Liquid_fluorine_tighter_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div>Liquid fluorine (at <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">extremely low temperatures</a>)</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#f0ff8f">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<div class="plainlist" style="margin: 0; text-align: left;"><ul><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ʊər/: 'our' in 'tour'">ʊər</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>, <wbr/>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>, <wbr/>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">FLOOR</span>-een, -in, --yn</i></a>)</li><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">ɔːr</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>, <wbr/>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>, <wbr/>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">FLOHR</span>-een, -in, -yn</i></a>)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">Allotropes</a></th><td>
alpha, beta</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
gas: very pale yellow<br/>liquid: bright yellow<br/>solid: alpha is opaque, beta is transparent</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_fluorine" title="Template:Infobox fluorine"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_fluorine" title="Template talk:Infobox fluorine"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_fluorine&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Fluorine</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>F</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 9. It is the lightest <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogen</a> and exists as a highly toxic pale yellow <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic</a> gas at <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a>. As the most <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegative</a> element, it is extremely reactive, as it reacts with almost all other elements, except for helium and neon.
</p><p>Among the elements, fluorine ranks <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">24th in universal abundance and 13th in terrestrial abundance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">Fluorite</a>, the primary mineral source of fluorine which gave the element its name, was first described in 1529; as it was added to metal ores to lower their melting points for <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>, the Latin verb <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">fluo</i></i> meaning "flow" gave the mineral its name. Proposed as an element in 1810, fluorine proved difficult and dangerous to separate from its compounds, and several early experimenters died or sustained injuries from their attempts. Only in 1886 did French chemist <a href="/wiki/Henri_Moissan" title="Henri Moissan">Henri Moissan</a> isolate elemental fluorine using low-temperature <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, a process still employed for modern production. Industrial production of fluorine gas for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">uranium enrichment</a>, its largest application, began during the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
</p><p>Owing to the expense of refining pure fluorine, most commercial applications use fluorine compounds, with about half of mined fluorite used in <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steelmaking</a>. The rest of the fluorite is converted into corrosive <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> en route to various organic fluorides, or into <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a> which plays a key role in <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">aluminium refining</a>. Organic fluorides have very high chemical and thermal stability; their major uses are as <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a>, electrical insulation and cookware, the last as <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">PTFE</a> (Teflon). Pharmaceuticals such as <a href="/wiki/Atorvastatin" title="Atorvastatin">atorvastatin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">fluoxetine</a> also contain fluorine, and the <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride ion</a> inhibits dental cavities, and so finds use in toothpaste and <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" title="Water fluoridation">water fluoridation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fluorochemical_industry" title="Fluorochemical industry">Global fluorochemical</a> sales amount to more than <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>15 billion a year.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">Fluorocarbon</a> gases are generally <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global-warming_potential" title="Global-warming potential">global-warming potentials</a> 100 to 20,000 times that of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organofluorine_compound" title="Organofluorine compound">Organofluorine compounds</a> persist in the environment due to the strength of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93fluorine_bond" title="Carbon–fluorine bond">carbon–fluorine bond</a>. Fluorine has no known metabolic role in mammals; a few plants synthesize organofluorine poisons that deter herbivores.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_configuration">Electron configuration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Electron configuration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fluorine_shielding.svg"><img alt="Two concentric rings showing valence and non-valence electron shells" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="350" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Fluorine_shielding.svg/220px-Fluorine_shielding.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Fluorine_shielding.svg/330px-Fluorine_shielding.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Fluorine_shielding.svg/440px-Fluorine_shielding.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fluorine_shielding.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simplified structure of the fluorine atom</div></div></div>
<p>Fluorine atoms have nine electrons, one fewer than <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> 1s<sup>2</sup>2s<sup>2</sup>2p<sup>5</sup>: two electrons in a filled inner shell and seven in an outer shell requiring one more to be filled. The outer electrons are ineffective at nuclear <a href="/wiki/Shielding_effect" title="Shielding effect">shielding</a>, and experience a high <a href="/wiki/Effective_nuclear_charge" title="Effective nuclear charge">effective nuclear charge</a> of 9 − 2 = 7; this affects the atom's physical properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000381_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000381-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fluorine's <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">first ionization energy</a> is third-highest among all elements, behind helium and neon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean1999564_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean1999564-13">[13]</a></sup> which complicates the removal of electrons from neutral fluorine atoms. It also has a high <a href="/wiki/Electron_affinity" title="Electron affinity">electron affinity</a>, second only to <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide200410.137–10.138_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide200410.137–10.138-14">[14]</a></sup> and tends to capture an electron to become <a href="/wiki/Isoelectronicity" title="Isoelectronicity">isoelectronic</a> with the noble gas neon;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000381_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000381-2">[2]</a></sup> it has the highest <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of any element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Fluorine atoms have a small <a href="/wiki/Covalent_radius_of_fluorine" title="Covalent radius of fluorine">covalent radius</a> of around 60 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">picometers</a>, similar to those of its <a href="/wiki/Period_2_element" title="Period 2 element">period</a> neighbors oxygen and neon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSD_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSD-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[note 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactivity">Reactivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Reactivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_fluorine#Chemical_characteristics_of_fluorine" title="Compounds of fluorine">Chemical characteristics of fluorine</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bond_energy" title="Bond energy">bond energy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_fluorine#Difluorine" title="Compounds of fluorine">difluorine</a> is much lower than that of either <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> or <span class="chemf nowrap">Br<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and similar to the easily cleaved <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxide</a> bond; this, along with high electronegativity, accounts for fluorine's easy dissociation, high reactivity, and strong bonds to non-fluorine atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998804_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998804-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Conversely, bonds to other atoms are very strong because of fluorine's high electronegativity. Unreactive substances like powdered steel, glass fragments, and <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> fibers react quickly with cold fluorine gas; wood and water spontaneously combust under a fluorine jet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_mechanics_F_reactivity_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pop_mechanics_F_reactivity-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 240px; float: left; clear: left; margin:0 1.5em 1.5em 0;"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vtWp45Eewtw" rel="nofollow">Bright flames</a> during fluorine reactions</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> Fluorine <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TLOFaWdPxB0" rel="nofollow">reacting with caesium</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Reactions of elemental fluorine with metals require varying conditions. <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">Alkali metals</a> cause explosions and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a> display vigorous activity in bulk; to prevent <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a> from the formation of metal fluoride layers, most other metals such as aluminium and iron must be powdered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998804_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998804-19">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Noble_metal" title="Noble metal">noble metals</a> require pure fluorine gas at 300–450 °C (575–850 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELidinMolochkoAndreeva2000442–455_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELidinMolochkoAndreeva2000442–455-22">[21]</a></sup> Some solid nonmetals (sulfur, phosphorus) react vigorously in liquid air temperature fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001404_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001404-23">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">Hydrogen sulfide</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001404_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001404-23">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatnaik2007472_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatnaik2007472-24">[23]</a></sup> combine readily with fluorine, the latter sometimes explosively; <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> exhibits much less activity, requiring elevated temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000400_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000400-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</a>, like some of the alkali metals, reacts explosively with fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw199876,_804_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw199876,_804-26">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a>, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamp_black" title="Lamp black">lamp black</a>, reacts at room temperature to yield <a href="/wiki/Fluoromethane" title="Fluoromethane">fluoromethane</a>. Graphite combines with fluorine above 400 °C (750 °F) to produce <a href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric_compound" title="Non-stoichiometric compound">non-stoichiometric</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monofluoride" title="Carbon monofluoride">carbon monofluoride</a>; higher temperatures generate gaseous <a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">fluorocarbons</a>, sometimes with explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuriakose,_A._K._27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuriakose,_A._K.-27">[26]</a></sup> Carbon dioxide and carbon monoxide react at or just above room temperature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hasegawa,_Yasuo_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hasegawa,_Yasuo-28">[27]</a></sup> whereas <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffins</a> and other organic chemicals generate strong reactions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> even fully substituted <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">haloalkanes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a>, normally incombustible, may explode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarrini,_Walter_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Navarrini,_Walter-30">[29]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trifluoride" title="Nitrogen trifluoride">nitrogen trifluoride</a> is stable, nitrogen requires an <a href="/wiki/Electric_discharge_in_gases" title="Electric discharge in gases">electric discharge</a> at elevated temperatures for reaction with fluorine to occur, due to the very strong <a href="/wiki/Triple_bond" title="Triple bond">triple bond</a> in elemental nitrogen;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELidinMolochkoAndreeva2000252_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELidinMolochkoAndreeva2000252-31">[30]</a></sup> ammonia may react explosively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anhydrous_ammonia_MSDS_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anhydrous_ammonia_MSDS-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_I._Morrow_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_I._Morrow-33">[32]</a></sup> Oxygen does not combine with fluorine under ambient conditions, but can be made to react using electric discharge at low temperatures and pressures; the products tend to disintegrate into their constituent elements when heated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emeléus_Sharpe_1974_111_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emeléus_Sharpe_1974_111-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001457_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001457-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Heavier halogens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000383_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000383-37">[36]</a></sup> react readily with fluorine as does the noble gas <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> of the other noble gases, only <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a> react, and only under special conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khriachtchev_et_al._2000_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khriachtchev_et_al._2000-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases">Phases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Phases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_fluorine" title="Phases of fluorine">Phases of fluorine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg"><img alt="Cube with spherical shapes on the corners and center and spinning molecules in planes in faces" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="406" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg/220px-Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg/330px-Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg/440px-Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beta_fluorine_unit_cell.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystal structure of β-fluorine. Spheres indicate <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules that may assume any angle. Other molecules are constrained to planes.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="640" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif/220px-Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif/330px-Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif/440px-Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beta-fluorine_crystal_structure.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation showing the crystal structure of beta-fluorine. Molecules on the faces of the unit cell have rotations constrained to a plane.</div></div></div>
<p>At room temperature, fluorine is a gas of <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic molecules</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382-4">[4]</a></sup> pale yellow when pure (sometimes described as yellow-green).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> It has a characteristic pungent odor detectable at 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.12_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.12-41">[40]</a></sup> Fluorine condenses into a bright yellow liquid at −188 °C (−306 °F), a transition temperature similar to those of oxygen and nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean1999523_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean1999523-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fluorine has two solid forms, α- and β-fluorine. The latter crystallizes at −220 °C (−364 °F) and is transparent and soft, with the same disordered <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic</a> structure of freshly crystallized solid oxygen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean1999523_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean1999523-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[note 2]</a></sup> unlike the <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">orthorhombic</a> systems of other solid halogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung197510_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung197510-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrett_1967_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett_1967-48">[46]</a></sup> Further cooling to −228 °C (−378 °F) induces a <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> into opaque and hard α-fluorine, which has a <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a> structure with dense, angled layers of molecules. The transition from β- to α-fluorine is more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> than the condensation of fluorine, and can be violent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung197510_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung197510-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrett_1967_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett_1967-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[note 3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_fluorine" title="Isotopes of fluorine">Isotopes of fluorine</a></div>
<p>Only one <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of fluorine occurs naturally in abundance, the stable isotope <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">19</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> It has a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetogyric_ratio" title="Magnetogyric ratio">magnetogyric ratio</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[note 4]</a></sup> and exceptional sensitivity to magnetic fields; because it is also <a href="/wiki/Monoisotopic_element" title="Monoisotopic element">the only stable isotope</a>, it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorine-19_nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Fluorine-19 nuclear magnetic resonance">used</a> in <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup> Seventeen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass numbers</a> from 14 to 31 have been synthesized, of which <a href="/wiki/Fluorine-18" title="Fluorine-18"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span></a> is the most stable with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 109.77 minutes. Other radioisotopes have half-lives less than 70 seconds; most decay in less than half a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotopes_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-54">[50]</a></sup> The isotopes <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span> undergo <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">β<sup>+</sup> decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>, lighter isotopes decay by <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a>, and those heavier than <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">19</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span> undergo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_minus_decay" title="Beta minus decay">β<sup>−</sup> decay</a> (the heaviest ones with delayed <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotopes_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-54">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> Two <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable isomers</a> of fluorine are known, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18m</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span>, with a half-life of 162(7) nanoseconds, and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26m</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span>, with a half-life of 2.2(1) milliseconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origin_and_occurrence_of_fluorine" title="Origin and occurrence of fluorine">Origin and occurrence of fluorine</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universe">Universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:15px; margin-top:0; font-size:10pt; line-height:11pt;">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Among the lighter elements, fluorine's abundance value of 400 <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppb</a> (parts per billion) – 24th among elements in the universe – is exceptionally low: other elements from carbon to magnesium are twenty or more times as common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosswell_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crosswell-58">[54]</a></sup> This is because <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> processes bypass fluorine, and any fluorine atoms otherwise created have high <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_cross_section" title="Nuclear cross section">nuclear cross sections</a>, allowing further fusion with hydrogen or helium to generate oxygen or neon respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosswell_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crosswell-58">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond this transient existence, three explanations have been proposed for the presence of fluorine:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crosswell_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Crosswell-58">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milky_Way_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Milky_Way-60">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>during <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">type II supernovae</a>, bombardment of neon atoms by <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> could transmute them to fluorine;</li>
<li>the solar wind of <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet stars</a> could blow fluorine away from any hydrogen or helium atoms; or</li>
<li>fluorine is borne out on convection currents arising from fusion in <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a> stars.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth">Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_fluorite_production" title="List of countries by fluorite production">List of countries by fluorite production</a></div>
<p>Fluorine is the thirteenth most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">common element in Earth's crust</a> at 600–700 ppm (parts per million) by mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384-61">[57]</a></sup> Elemental fluorine in Earth's atmosphere would easily react with atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, precluding its natural occurrence;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> it is found only in combined mineral forms, of which <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorapatite" title="Fluorapatite">fluorapatite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a> are the most industrially significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384-61">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795-64">[60]</a></sup> Fluorite or fluorspar (<span class="chemf nowrap">CaF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), colorful and abundant worldwide, is fluorine's main source; China and Mexico are the major suppliers. The U.S. led extraction in the early 20th century but ceased mining in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KGS_fluorite_terminology_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-KGS_fluorite_terminology-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historical_fluorspar_statistics_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-historical_fluorspar_statistics-67">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_fluorspar_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK_fluorspar-68">[64]</a></sup> Although fluorapatite (Ca<sub>5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F) contains most of the world's fluorine, its low <a href="/wiki/Mass_fraction_(chemistry)" title="Mass fraction (chemistry)">mass fraction</a> of 3.5% means that most of it is used as a phosphate. In the U.S. small quantities of fluorine compounds are obtained via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorosilicic_acid" title="Fluorosilicic acid">fluorosilicic acid</a>, a phosphate industry byproduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795-64">[60]</a></sup> Cryolite (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>AlF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>), once used directly in aluminium production, is the rarest and most concentrated of these three minerals. The main commercial mine on Greenland's west coast closed in 1987, and most cryolite is now synthesized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795-64">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<table cellpadding="3" style="margin:1em auto 1em auto; text-align:center;">
<tbody><tr>
<td colspan="3" style="text-align:center;"><b>Major fluorine-containing minerals</b>
</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Other minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Topaz" title="Topaz">topaz</a> contain fluorine. Fluorides, unlike other halides, are insoluble and do not occur in commercially favorable concentrations in saline waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998795-64">[60]</a></sup> Trace quantities of organofluorines of uncertain origin have been detected in volcanic eruptions and geothermal springs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribble2002_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribble2002-69">[65]</a></sup> The existence of gaseous fluorine in crystals, suggested by the smell of crushed <a href="/wiki/Antozonite" title="Antozonite">antozonite</a>, is contentious;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elementar_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elementar-71">[67]</a></sup> a 2012 study reported the presence of 0.04% <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> by weight in antozonite, attributing these <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(mineral)" title="Inclusion (mineral)">inclusions</a> to radiation from the presence of tiny amounts of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elementar_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elementar-71">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_fluorine" title="History of fluorine">History of fluorine</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_discoveries">Early discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Book9-25.gif"><img alt="Woodcut image showing man at open hearth with tongs and machine bellows to the side in background, man at water-operated hammer with quenching sluice nearby in foreground" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1505" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Book9-25.gif/170px-Book9-25.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Book9-25.gif/255px-Book9-25.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Book9-25.gif/340px-Book9-25.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Book9-25.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steelmaking illustration from <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_metallica" title="De re metallica">De re metallica</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In 1529, <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a> described fluorite as an additive used to lower the melting point of metals during <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998790_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998790-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assassinated_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-assassinated-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[note 5]</a></sup> He penned the Latin word <i>fluorés</i> (<i>fluor,</i> flow) for fluorite rocks. The name later evolved into <i>fluorspar</i> (still commonly used) and then <i>fluorite</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KGS_fluorite_terminology_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-KGS_fluorite_terminology-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998109_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998109-78">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> The composition of fluorite was later determined to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_difluoride" title="Calcium difluoride">calcium difluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-80">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">Hydrofluoric acid</a> was used in <a href="/wiki/Glass_etching" title="Glass etching">glass etching</a> from 1720 onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[note 6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Sigismund Marggraf</a> first characterized it in 1764 when he heated fluorite with sulfuric acid, and the resulting solution corroded its glass container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup> Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> repeated the experiment in 1771, and named the acidic product <i>fluss-spats-syran</i> (fluorspar acid).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup> In 1810, the French physicist <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">André-Marie Ampère</a> suggested that hydrogen and an element analogous to chlorine constituted hydrofluoric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Sir Humphry Davy</a> proposed that this then-unknown substance be named <i>fluorine</i> from fluoric acid and the <i>-ine</i> suffix of other halogens. This word, with modifications, is used in most European languages; Greek, Russian, and some others (following Ampère's suggestion) use the name <i>ftor</i> or derivatives, from the Greek φθόριος (<i>phthorios</i>, destructive).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavy1813[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddhdGAAAAMAAJpgPA278_278]_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavy1813[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiddhdGAAAAMAAJpgPA278_278]-87">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanks198611_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanks198611-88">[82]</a></sup> The New Latin name <i>fluorum</i> gave the element its current symbol <b>F</b>; Fl was used in early papers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dissolve_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-dissolve-89">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation">Isolation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Isolation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Initial studies on fluorine were so dangerous that several 19th-century experimenters were deemed "fluorine martyrs" after misfortunes with hydrofluoric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[note 8]</a></sup> Isolation of elemental fluorine was hindered by the extreme corrosiveness of both elemental fluorine itself and hydrogen fluoride, as well as the lack of a simple and suitable <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toon_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Toon-91">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Fr%C3%A9my" title="Edmond Frémy">Edmond Frémy</a> postulated that <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of pure hydrogen fluoride to generate fluorine was feasible and devised a method to produce anhydrous samples from acidified <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bifluoride" title="Potassium bifluoride">potassium bifluoride</a>; instead, he discovered that the resulting (dry) hydrogen fluoride <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">did not conduct</a> electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toon_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Toon-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-93">[85]</a></sup> Frémy's former student <a href="/wiki/Henri_Moissan" title="Henri Moissan">Henri Moissan</a> persevered, and after much trial and error found that a mixture of potassium bifluoride and dry hydrogen fluoride was a conductor, enabling electrolysis. To prevent rapid corrosion of the platinum in his <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_cell" title="Electrochemical cell">electrochemical cells</a>, he cooled the reaction to extremely low temperatures in a special bath and forged cells from a more resistant mixture of platinum and <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, and used fluorite stoppers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toon_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-Toon-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998789–791_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998789–791-94">[86]</a></sup> In 1886, after 74 years of effort by many chemists, Moissan isolated elemental fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-93">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qwen_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-qwen-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1906, two months before his death, Moissan received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chem_Nobel_laureates_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chem_Nobel_laureates-96">[88]</a></sup> with the following citation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toon_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-Toon-91">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[I]n recognition of the great services rendered by him in his investigation and isolation of the element fluorine ... The whole world has admired the great experimental skill with which you have studied that savage beast among the elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[note 9]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 174.66666666667px"><div style="width: 174.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 172.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor,_Fig._5.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="590" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG/259px-Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG/389px-Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG/519px-Recherches_sur_l%E2%80%99isolement_du_fluor%2C_Fig._5.PNG 2x" width="173"/></a></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_uses">Later uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Later uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="683" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/220px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/330px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/440px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ampoule of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> or <i>hex</i></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Frigidaire" title="Frigidaire">Frigidaire</a> division of <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> (GM) experimented with chlorofluorocarbon refrigerants in the late 1920s, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinetic_Chemicals" title="Kinetic Chemicals">Kinetic Chemicals</a> was formed as a joint venture between GM and <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> in 1930 hoping to market Freon-12 (<span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">CCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></a></span>) as one such <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerant</a>. It replaced earlier and more toxic compounds, increased demand for kitchen refrigerators, and became profitable; by 1949 DuPont had bought out Kinetic and marketed several other <a href="/wiki/Freon" title="Freon">Freon</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jstg_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jstg-98">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHounshellSmith1988156–157_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHounshellSmith1988156–157-99">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD_Freon_history_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-DD_Freon_history-100">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">Polytetrafluoroethylene</a> (Teflon) was serendipitously discovered in 1938 by <a href="/wiki/Roy_J._Plunkett" title="Roy J. Plunkett">Roy J. Plunkett</a> while working on refrigerants at Kinetic, and its superlative chemical and thermal resistance lent it to accelerated commercialization and mass production by 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jstg_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jstg-98">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHounshellSmith1988156–157_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHounshellSmith1988156–157-99">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Large-scale production of elemental fluorine began during World War II. Germany used high-temperature electrolysis to make tons of the planned incendiary chlorine trifluoride<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[92]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> used huge quantities to produce <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> for uranium enrichment. Since <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> is as corrosive as fluorine, <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_diffusion" title="Gaseous diffusion">gaseous diffusion</a> plants required special materials: nickel for membranes, fluoropolymers for seals, and liquid fluorocarbons as coolants and lubricants. This burgeoning nuclear industry later drove post-war fluorochemical development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_fluorine" title="Compounds of fluorine">Compounds of fluorine</a></div>
<p>Fluorine has a rich chemistry, encompassing organic and inorganic domains.  It combines with metals, nonmetals, <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a>, and most noble gases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedel_Kaupp_2009_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedel_Kaupp_2009-103">[94]</a></sup> and usually assumes an <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of −1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[note 10]</a></sup>  Fluorine's high electron affinity results in a preference for <a href="/wiki/Ionic_bonding" title="Ionic bonding">ionic bonding</a>; when it forms <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>, these are polar, and almost always <a href="/wiki/Single_bond" title="Single bond">single</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NF_charge_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-NF_charge-107">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[note 11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metals">Metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fluoride_volatility" title="Fluoride volatility">Fluoride volatility</a></div>
<p>Alkali metals form ionic and highly soluble <a href="/wiki/Monofluoride" title="Monofluoride">monofluorides</a>; these have the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system#Rock-salt_structure" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic arrangement of sodium chloride</a> and analogous chlorides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000420–422_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000420–422-111">[100]</a></sup> Alkaline earth <a href="/wiki/Difluoride" title="Difluoride">difluorides</a> possess strong ionic bonds but are insoluble in water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dissolve_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-dissolve-89">[83]</a></sup> with the exception of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beryllium_difluoride" title="Beryllium difluoride">beryllium difluoride</a>, which also exhibits some covalent character and has a <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">quartz</a>-like structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beryllium_chemistry_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beryllium_chemistry-112">[101]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">Rare earth elements</a> and many other metals form mostly ionic <a href="/wiki/Trifluoride" title="Trifluoride">trifluorides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmeléusSharpe198389–97_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmeléusSharpe198389–97-113">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crystal_chemistry_of_fluorides_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crystal_chemistry_of_fluorides-114">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Covalent bonding first comes to prominence in the <a href="/wiki/Tetrafluoride" title="Tetrafluoride">tetrafluorides</a>: those of zirconium, hafnium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_2011_193_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perry_2011_193-117">[106]</a></sup> and several actinides<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[107]</a></sup> are ionic with high melting points,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.60,_4.76,_4.92,_4.96_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.60,_4.76,_4.92,_4.96-119">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[note 12]</a></sup> while those of titanium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998964_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998964-123">[111]</a></sup> vanadium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[112]</a></sup> and niobium are polymeric,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998990_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998990-125">[113]</a></sup> melting or decomposing at no more than 350 °C (660 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.72,_4.91,_4.93_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.72,_4.91,_4.93-126">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pentafluoride" title="Pentafluoride">Pentafluorides</a> continue this trend with their linear polymers and <a href="/wiki/Oligomer" title="Oligomer">oligomeric</a> complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998561–563_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998561–563-127">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmeléusSharpe1983256–277_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmeléusSharpe1983256–277-128">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackayMackayHenderson2002355–356_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackayMackayHenderson2002355–356-129">[117]</a></sup> Thirteen metal <a href="/wiki/Hexafluoride" title="Hexafluoride">hexafluorides</a> are known,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[note 13]</a></sup> all octahedral, and are mostly volatile solids but for liquid <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Molybdenum_hexafluoride" title="Molybdenum hexafluoride">MoF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></a></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Rhenium_hexafluoride" title="Rhenium hexafluoride">ReF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></a></span>, and gaseous <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Tungsten_hexafluoride" title="Tungsten hexafluoride">WF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998(various_pages,_by_metal_in_respective_chapters)_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998(various_pages,_by_metal_in_respective_chapters)-131">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.71,_4.78,_4.92_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.71,_4.78,_4.92-132">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D_BLOCK_XF6_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-D_BLOCK_XF6-133">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rhenium_heptafluoride" title="Rhenium heptafluoride">Rhenium heptafluoride</a>, the only characterized metal <a href="/wiki/Heptafluoride" title="Heptafluoride">heptafluoride</a>, is a low-melting molecular solid with <a href="/wiki/Pentagonal_bipyramidal_molecular_geometry" title="Pentagonal bipyramidal molecular geometry">pentagonal bipyramidal molecular geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998819_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998819-134">[121]</a></sup> Metal fluorides with more fluorine atoms are particularly reactive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<table cellpadding="3" style="margin:1em auto 1em auto; text-align:center;">
