<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">Hydrogen fluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg"><img alt="Graph showing water and hydrogen fluoride breaking the trend of lower boiling points for lighter molecules" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="337" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg/220px-Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg/330px-Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg/440px-Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boiling-points_Chalcogen-Halogen.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boiling points of hydrogen halides and chalcogenides, showing the unusually high values for hydrogen fluoride and water</div></div></div>
<p>Hydrogen and fluorine combine to yield hydrogen fluoride, in which discrete molecules form clusters by hydrogen bonding, resembling water more than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauling_HF_hydrogen_bonds_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pauling_HF_hydrogen_bonds-136">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_HF_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins_HF-137">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Scientist_HF_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist_HF-138">[125]</a></sup> It boils at a much higher temperature than heavier hydrogen halides and unlike them is fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscible" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998812–816_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998812–816-139">[126]</a></sup> Hydrogen fluoride readily hydrates on contact with water to form aqueous hydrogen fluoride, also known as hydrofluoric acid. Unlike the other hydrohalic acids, which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_acids" title="Strong acids">strong</a>, hydrofluoric acid is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_acid" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a> at low concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001425_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001425-140">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[note 14]</a></sup> However, it can attack glass, something the other acids cannot do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H+_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-H+-143">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_reactive_nonmetals">Other reactive nonmetals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other reactive nonmetals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>Metalloids are included in this section</i></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="1100" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png/220px-Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png/330px-Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png/440px-Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chlorine-trifluoride-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chlorine_trifluoride" title="Chlorine trifluoride">Chlorine trifluoride</a>, whose corrosive potential ignites asbestos, concrete, sand and other fire retardants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAir_Products_and_Chemicals20041_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAir_Products_and_Chemicals20041-144">[130]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Binary fluorides of metalloids and p-block nonmetals are generally covalent and volatile, with varying reactivities. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_3" title="Period 3">Period 3</a> and heavier nonmetals can form <a href="/wiki/Hypervalent_molecule" title="Hypervalent molecule">hypervalent</a> fluorides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noury_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noury-145">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Boron_trifluoride" title="Boron trifluoride">Boron trifluoride</a> is planar and possesses an incomplete octet. It functions as a <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acids_and_bases" title="Lewis acids and bases">Lewis acid</a> and combines with Lewis bases like ammonia to form <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adducts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[132]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrafluoride" title="Carbon tetrafluoride">Carbon tetrafluoride</a> is tetrahedral and inert;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[note 15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">its group</a> analogues, silicon and germanium tetrafluoride, are also tetrahedral<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[133]</a></sup> but behave as Lewis acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000423_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000423-149">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001897_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001897-150">[135]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogens</a> form trifluorides that increase in reactivity and basicity with higher molecular weight, although <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trifluoride" title="Nitrogen trifluoride">nitrogen trifluoride</a> resists hydrolysis and is not basic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PnFx_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-PnFx-151">[136]</a></sup> The pentafluorides of phosphorus, arsenic, and antimony are more reactive than their respective trifluorides, with <a href="/wiki/Antimony_pentafluoride" title="Antimony pentafluoride">antimony pentafluoride</a> the strongest neutral Lewis acid known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998561–563_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998561–563-127">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arsenic_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arsenic-152">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000432_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000432-153">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">Chalcogens</a> have diverse fluorides: unstable difluorides have been reported for oxygen (the only known compound with oxygen in an oxidation state of +2), sulfur, and selenium; tetrafluorides and hexafluorides exist for sulfur, selenium, and tellurium. The latter are stabilized by more fluorine atoms and lighter central atoms, so <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> is especially inert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-(O,S,Se,Te)Fn_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-(O,S,Se,Te)Fn-154">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998638–640,_683–689,_767–778_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998638–640,_683–689,_767–778-155">[140]</a></sup> Chlorine, bromine, and iodine can each form mono-, tri-, and pentafluorides, but only <a href="/wiki/Iodine_heptafluoride" title="Iodine heptafluoride">iodine heptafluoride</a> has been characterized among possible <a href="/wiki/Interhalogen" title="Interhalogen">interhalogen</a> heptafluorides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001435–436_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001435–436-156">[141]</a></sup> Many of them are powerful sources of fluorine atoms, and industrial applications using chlorine trifluoride require precautions similar to those using fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998828–830_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998828–830-157">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patnaik_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patnaik-158">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noble_gases">Noble gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Noble gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas_compound" title="Noble gas compound">Noble gas compound</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif"><img alt="Black-and-white photo showing transparent crystals in a dish" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif/220px-Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif/330px-Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b2/Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_tetrafluoride_crop.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These xenon tetrafluoride crystals were photographed in 1962. The compound's synthesis, as with xenon hexafluoroplatinate, surprised many chemists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoellerBailarKleinberg1980236_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoellerBailarKleinberg1980236-159">[144]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">Noble gases</a>, having complete electron shells, defied reaction with other elements until 1962 when <a href="/wiki/Neil_Bartlett_(chemist)" title="Neil Bartlett (chemist)">Neil Bartlett</a> reported synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Xenon_hexafluoroplatinate" title="Xenon hexafluoroplatinate">xenon hexafluoroplatinate</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001392–393_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001392–393-160">[145]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Xenon_difluoride" title="Xenon difluoride">xenon difluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenon_tetrafluoride" title="Xenon tetrafluoride">tetrafluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenon_hexafluoride" title="Xenon hexafluoride">hexafluoride</a>, and multiple oxyfluorides have been isolated since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001395–397,_400_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001395–397,_400-161">[146]</a></sup> Among other noble gases, krypton forms a <a href="/wiki/Krypton_difluoride" title="Krypton difluoride">difluoride</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewars200868_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewars200868-162">[147]</a></sup> and radon and fluorine generate a solid suspected to be <a href="/wiki/Radon_difluoride" title="Radon difluoride">radon difluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astatineradon_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-astatineradon-163">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewars200867_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewars200867-164">[149]</a></sup> Binary fluorides of lighter noble gases are exceptionally unstable: argon and hydrogen fluoride combine under extreme conditions to give <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">argon fluorohydride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khriachtchev_et_al._2000_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Khriachtchev_et_al._2000-39">[38]</a></sup> Helium and neon have no long-lived fluorides,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeNeCompounds_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeNeCompounds-165">[150]</a></sup> and no neon fluoride has ever been observed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewars200871_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewars200871-166">[151]</a></sup> helium fluorohydride has been detected for milliseconds at high pressures and low temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeNeCompounds_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-HeNeCompounds-165">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds">Organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organofluorine_chemistry" title="Organofluorine chemistry">Organofluorine chemistry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG"><img alt="Beaker with two layers of liquid, goldfish and crab in top, coin sunk in the bottom" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1892" data-file-width="2574" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG/220px-FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG/330px-FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG/440px-FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FluorocarbonCrabFish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">Immiscible</a> layers of colored water (top) and much denser <a href="/wiki/Perfluoroheptane" title="Perfluoroheptane">perfluoroheptane</a> (bottom) in a beaker; a goldfish and crab cannot penetrate the boundary; <a href="/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin)" title="Quarter (United States coin)">quarters</a> rest at the bottom.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nafion2.svg"><img alt="Skeletal chemical formula" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="1063" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Nafion2.svg/220px-Nafion2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Nafion2.svg/330px-Nafion2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Nafion2.svg/440px-Nafion2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nafion2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chemical structure of <a href="/wiki/Nafion" title="Nafion">Nafion</a>, a fluoropolymer used in fuel cells and many other applications<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogers20044–12_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogers20044–12-167">[152]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93fluorine_bond" title="Carbon–fluorine bond">carbon–fluorine bond</a> is <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>'s strongest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagan_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagan-168">[153]</a></sup> and gives stability to organofluorines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiegemund_et_al.2005444_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiegemund_et_al.2005444-169">[154]</a></sup> It is almost non-existent in nature, but is used in artificial compounds. Research in this area is usually driven by commercial applications;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[155]</a></sup> the compounds involved are diverse and reflect the complexity inherent in organic chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jstg_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jstg-98">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Discrete_molecules">Discrete molecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Discrete molecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">Fluorocarbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perfluorinated_compound" title="Perfluorinated compound">Perfluorinated compound</a></div>
<p>The substitution of hydrogen atoms in an <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a> by progressively more fluorine atoms gradually alters several properties: melting and boiling points are lowered, density increases, solubility in hydrocarbons decreases and overall stability increases. Perfluorocarbons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[note 16]</a></sup> in which all hydrogen atoms are substituted, are insoluble in most organic solvents, reacting at ambient conditions only with sodium in liquid ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiegemund_et_al.2005451–452_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiegemund_et_al.2005451–452-172">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Perfluorinated_compound" title="Perfluorinated compound">perfluorinated compound</a></i> is used for what would otherwise be a perfluorocarbon if not for the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[note 17]</a></sup> often a <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a>. These compounds share many properties with perfluorocarbons such as stability and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobicity" title="Hydrophobicity">hydrophobicity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorotelomer_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-fluorotelomer-176">[159]</a></sup> while the functional group augments their reactivity, enabling them to adhere to surfaces or act as <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">surfactants</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salager2002_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salager2002-177">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fluorosurfactant" title="Fluorosurfactant">Fluorosurfactants</a>, in particular,  can lower the <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> of water more than their hydrocarbon-based analogues. <a href="/wiki/Fluorotelomer" title="Fluorotelomer">Fluorotelomers</a>, which have some unfluorinated carbon atoms near the functional group, are also regarded as perfluorinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorotelomer_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-fluorotelomer-176">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymers">Polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Polymers exhibit the same stability increases afforded by fluorine substitution (for hydrogen) in discrete molecules; their melting points generally increase too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053-178">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" title="Polytetrafluoroethylene">Polytetrafluoroethylene</a> (PTFE), the simplest fluoropolymer and perfluoro analogue of <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">polyethylene</a> with <a href="/wiki/Structural_unit" title="Structural unit">structural unit</a> –<span class="chemf nowrap">CF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>–, demonstrates this change as expected, but its very high melting point makes it difficult to mold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053–4_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053–4-179">[162]</a></sup> Various PTFE derivatives are less temperature-tolerant but easier to mold: <a href="/wiki/Fluorinated_ethylene_propylene" title="Fluorinated ethylene propylene">fluorinated ethylene propylene</a> replaces some fluorine atoms with <a href="/wiki/Trifluoromethyl" title="Trifluoromethyl">trifluoromethyl</a> groups, <a href="/wiki/Perfluoroalkoxy_alkane" title="Perfluoroalkoxy alkane">perfluoroalkoxy alkanes</a> do the same with <a href="/wiki/Trifluoromethoxy" title="Trifluoromethoxy">trifluoromethoxy</a> groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053–4_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053–4-179">[162]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nafion" title="Nafion">Nafion</a> contains perfluoroether side chains capped with <a href="/wiki/Sulfonic_acid" title="Sulfonic acid">sulfonic acid</a> groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[164]</a></sup> Other fluoropolymers retain some hydrogen atoms; <a href="/wiki/Polyvinylidene_fluoride" title="Polyvinylidene fluoride">polyvinylidene fluoride</a> has half the fluorine atoms of PTFE and <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_fluoride" title="Polyvinyl fluoride">polyvinyl fluoride</a> has a quarter, but both behave much like perfluorinated polymers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20054_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20054-182">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fluorine_cell_room.jpg"><img alt="A machine room" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="480" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fluorine_cell_room.jpg/220px-Fluorine_cell_room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fluorine_cell_room.jpg/330px-Fluorine_cell_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fluorine_cell_room.jpg/440px-Fluorine_cell_room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fluorine_cell_room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Industrial fluorine cells at <a href="/wiki/City_of_Preston,_Lancashire" title="City of Preston, Lancashire">Preston</a></div></div></div>
<p>Moissan's method is used to produce industrial quantities of fluorine, via the electrolysis of a <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">potassium fluoride</a>/<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> mixture: hydrogen and fluoride ions are reduced and oxidized at a steel container <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a> and a carbon block <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>, under 8–12 volts, to generate hydrogen and fluorine gas respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000386_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000386-183">[166]</a></sup> Temperatures are elevated, KF•2HF melting at 70 °C (158 °F) and being electrolyzed at 70–130 °C (158–266 °F). KF, which acts as catalyst, is essential since pure HF cannot be electrolyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384–285_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384–285-184">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998796–797_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1998796–797-185">[168]</a></sup> Fluorine can be stored in steel cylinders that have passivated interiors, at temperatures below 200 °C (392 °F); otherwise nickel can be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384–385_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000384–385-186">[169]</a></sup> Regulator valves and pipework are made of nickel, the latter possibly using <a href="/wiki/Monel" title="Monel">Monel</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000390–391_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000390–391-187">[170]</a></sup> Frequent passivation, along with the strict exclusion of water and greases, must be undertaken. In the laboratory, glassware may carry fluorine gas under low pressure and anhydrous conditions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000390–391_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000390–391-187">[170]</a></sup> some sources instead recommend nickel-Monel-PTFE systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shriver_Inorganic_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shriver_Inorganic-188">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While preparing for a 1986 conference to celebrate the centennial of Moissan's achievement, <a href="/wiki/Karl_O._Christe" title="Karl O. Christe">Karl O. Christe</a> reasoned that chemical fluorine generation should be feasible since some metal fluoride anions have no stable neutral counterparts; their acidification potentially triggers oxidation instead. He devised a method which evolves fluorine at high yield and atmospheric pressure:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-year_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-year-189">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_permanganate" title="Potassium permanganate">KMnO<sub>4</sub></a> + 2 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">KF</a> + 10 <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">HF</a> + 3 <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub></a> → 2 K<sub>2</sub>MnF<sub>6</sub> + 8 <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">H<sub>2</sub>O</a> + 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">O<sub>2</sub></a>↑</dd>
<dd>2 K<sub>2</sub>MnF<sub>6</sub> + 4 <a href="/wiki/Antimony_pentafluoride" title="Antimony pentafluoride">SbF<sub>5</sub></a> → 4 KSbF<sub>6</sub> + 2 MnF<sub>3</sub> + F<sub>2</sub>↑</dd></dl>
<p>Christe later commented that the reactants "had been known for more than 100 years and even Moissan could have come up with this scheme."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[173]</a></sup> As late as 2008, some references still asserted that fluorine was too reactive for any chemical isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_applications">Industrial applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Industrial applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fluorochemical_industry" title="Fluorochemical industry">Fluorochemical industry</a></div>
<p>Fluorite mining, which supplies most global fluorine, peaked in 1989 when 5.6 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> of ore were extracted. Chlorofluorocarbon restrictions lowered this to 3.6 million tons in 1994; production has since been increasing. Around 4.5 million tons of ore and revenue of <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>550 million were generated in 2003; later reports estimated 2011 global fluorochemical sales at $15 billion and predicted 2016–18 production figures of 3.5 to 5.9 million tons, and revenue of at least $20 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">Froth flotation</a> separates mined fluorite into two main metallurgical grades of equal proportion: 60–85% pure metspar is almost all used in iron smelting whereas 97%+ pure acidspar is mainly converted to the key industrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_intermediate" title="Chemical intermediate">intermediate</a> hydrogen fluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ModFcchem_84-7"><a href="#cite_note-ModFcchem-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SME_mining_2006_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-SME_mining_2006-196">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:677px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_699545218"><area alt="Fluorite" coords="10,6,87,37"
