hexafluoroaluminate"/><area alt="Pickling (metal)" coords="336,131,447,166" href="/wiki/Pickling_(metal)" shape="rect" title="Pickling (metal)"/><area alt="Fluorosilicic acid" coords="335,185,445,216" href="/wiki/Hexafluorosilicic_acid" shape="rect" title="Fluorosilicic acid"/><area alt="Alkane cracking" coords="334,228,445,262" href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" shape="rect" title="Alkane cracking"/><area alt="Hydrofluorocarbon" coords="522,6,632,37" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" shape="rect" title="Hydrofluorocarbon"/><area alt="Hydrochlorofluorocarbons" coords="523,51,632,82" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon#Development_of_alternatives_for_CFCs" shape="rect" title="Hydrochlorofluorocarbons"/><area alt="Chlorofluorocarbon" coords="522,95,633,125" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" shape="rect" title="Chlorofluorocarbon"/><area alt="Teflon" coords="522,138,632,173" href="/wiki/Polytetrafluoroethylene" shape="rect" title="Teflon"/><area alt="Water fluoridation" coords="523,184,633,216" href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" shape="rect" title="Water fluoridation"/><area alt="Uranium enrichment" coords="522,227,633,257" href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" shape="rect" title="Uranium enrichment"/><area alt="Sulfur hexafluoride" coords="523,279,633,310" href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" shape="rect" title="Sulfur hexafluoride"/><area alt="Tungsten hexafluoride" coords="523,321,632,386" href="/wiki/Tungsten_hexafluoride" shape="rect" title="Tungsten hexafluoride"/><area alt="Phosphogypsum" coords="30,266,191,316" href="/wiki/Phosphogypsum" shape="rect" title="Phosphogypsum"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="363" data-file-width="625" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_fluorine_economy.svg/675px-The_fluorine_economy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_fluorine_economy.svg/1013px-The_fluorine_economy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_fluorine_economy.svg/1350px-The_fluorine_economy.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_699545218" width="675"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_fluorine_economy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clickable diagram of the fluorochemical industry according to mass flows</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg"><img alt="Minaret-like electrical devices with wires around them, thicker at the bottom" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg/220px-SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg/330px-SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg/440px-SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SF6_current_transformer_TGFM-110_Russia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="chemf nowrap">SF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> transformers at a Russian railway</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_gas" title="Industrial gas">Industrial gas</a></div>
<p>At least 17,000 metric tons of fluorine are produced each year. It costs only $5–8 per kilogram as uranium or sulfur hexafluoride, but many times more as an element because of handling challenges. Most processes using free fluorine in large amounts employ <i>in situ</i> generation under <a href="/wiki/Vertical_integration" title="Vertical integration">vertical integration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000392_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000392-197">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest application of fluorine gas, consuming up to 7,000 metric tons annually, is in the preparation of <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a>. Fluorine is used to fluorinate <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride">uranium tetrafluoride</a>, itself formed from uranium dioxide and hydrofluoric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000392_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000392-197">[180]</a></sup> Fluorine is monoisotopic, so any mass differences between <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> molecules are due to the presence of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">235</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>U</span> or <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">238</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>U</span>, enabling uranium enrichment via gaseous diffusion or <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">gas centrifuge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000382-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup> About 6,000 metric tons per year go into producing the inert <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">SF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> for high-voltage transformers and circuit breakers, eliminating the need for hazardous <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyls</a> associated with <span class="nowrap">oil-filled</span> devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000430_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000430-198">[181]</a></sup> Several fluorine compounds are used in electronics: rhenium and tungsten hexafluoride in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">chemical vapor deposition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetrafluoromethane" title="Tetrafluoromethane">tetrafluoromethane</a> in <a href="/wiki/Plasma_etching" title="Plasma etching">plasma etching</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000391–392_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaccaud_et_al.2000391–392-199">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[184]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trifluoride" title="Nitrogen trifluoride">nitrogen trifluoride</a> in cleaning equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup> Fluorine is also used in the synthesis of organic fluorides, but its reactivity often necessitates conversion first to the gentler <span class="chemf nowrap">ClF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">BrF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, or <span class="chemf nowrap">IF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, which together allow calibrated fluorination. Fluorinated pharmaceuticals use <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_tetrafluoride" title="Sulfur tetrafluoride">sulfur tetrafluoride</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-5"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_fluorides">Inorganic fluorides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Inorganic fluorides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="640" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg/220px-Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg/330px-Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg/440px-Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hall-Heroult_cell_schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aluminium extraction depends critically on cryolite</div></div></div>
<p>As with other iron alloys, around 3 kg (6.5 lb) metspar is added to each metric ton of steel; the fluoride ions lower its melting point and <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-6"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-202">[185]</a></sup> Alongside its role as an additive in materials like enamels and welding rod coats, most acidspar is reacted with sulfuric acid to form hydrofluoric acid, which is used in steel <a href="/wiki/Pickling_(metal)" title="Pickling (metal)">pickling</a>, glass etching and <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">alkane cracking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-7"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup> One-third of HF goes into synthesizing <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hexafluoroaluminate" title="Sodium hexafluoroaluminate">cryolite</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_trifluoride" title="Aluminium trifluoride">aluminium trifluoride</a>, both fluxes in the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a> for aluminium extraction; replenishment is necessitated by their occasional reactions with the smelting apparatus. Each metric ton of aluminium requires about 23 kg (51 lb) of flux.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-8"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[186]</a></sup> Fluorosilicates consume the second largest portion, with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluorosilicate" title="Sodium fluorosilicate">sodium fluorosilicate</a> used in water fluoridation and laundry effluent treatment, and as an intermediate en route to cryolite and silicon tetrafluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000428_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000428-204">[187]</a></sup> Other important inorganic fluorides include those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobalt_difluoride" title="Cobalt difluoride">cobalt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel_difluoride" title="Nickel difluoride">nickel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_fluoride" title="Ammonium fluoride">ammonium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-9"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000420–422_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigueperse_et_al.2000420–422-111">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_fluorides">Organic fluorides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Organic fluorides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organofluorides consume over 20% of mined fluorite and over 40% of hydrofluoric acid, with <a href="#Refrigerant_gases">refrigerant gases</a> dominating and <a href="#Fluoropolymers">fluoropolymers</a> increasing their market share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-10"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIA_f-chem_report_description_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIA_f-chem_report_description-206">[189]</a></sup> <a href="#Surfactants">Surfactants</a> are a minor application but generate over $1 billion in annual revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorosurfactant_market_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-fluorosurfactant_market-207">[190]</a></sup> Due to the danger from direct hydrocarbon–fluorine reactions above −150 °C (−240 °F), industrial fluorocarbon production is indirect, mostly through <a href="/wiki/Finkelstein_reaction" title="Finkelstein reaction">halogen exchange reactions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Swarts_fluorination" title="Swarts fluorination">Swarts fluorination</a>, in which chlorocarbon chlorines are substituted for fluorines by hydrogen fluoride under catalysts. <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_fluorination" title="Electrochemical fluorination">Electrochemical fluorination</a> subjects hydrocarbons to electrolysis in hydrogen fluoride, and the <a href="/wiki/Fowler_process" title="Fowler process">Fowler process</a> treats them with solid fluorine carriers like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobalt_trifluoride" title="Cobalt trifluoride">cobalt trifluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jstg_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jstg-98">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigerant_gases">Refrigerant gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Refrigerant gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">Refrigerant</a></div>
<p>Halogenated refrigerants, termed Freons in informal contexts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[note 18]</a></sup> are identified by <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant#Refrigerants_by_class_and_R-number" title="Refrigerant">R-numbers</a> that denote the amount of fluorine, chlorine, carbon, and hydrogen present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-11"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[192]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">Chlorofluorocarbons</a> (CFCs) like <a href="/wiki/Trichlorofluoromethane" title="Trichlorofluoromethane">R-11</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">R-12</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,2-dichlorotetrafluoroethane" title="1,2-dichlorotetrafluoroethane">R-114</a> once dominated organofluorines, peaking in production in the 1980s. Used for air conditioning systems, propellants and solvents, their production was below one-tenth of this peak by the early 2000s, after widespread international prohibition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-12"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup> Hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HCFCs) and hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) were designed as replacements; their synthesis consumes more than 90% of the fluorine in the organic industry. Important HCFCs include R-22, <a href="/wiki/Chlorodifluoromethane" title="Chlorodifluoromethane">chlorodifluoromethane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1,1-Dichloro-1-fluoroethane" title="1,1-Dichloro-1-fluoroethane">R-141b</a>. The main HFC is <a href="/wiki/1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane" title="1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane">R-134a</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AcountF_66-13"><a href="#cite_note-AcountF-66">[62]</a></sup> with a new type of molecule <a href="/wiki/2,3,3,3-Tetrafluoropropene" title="2,3,3,3-Tetrafluoropropene">HFO-1234yf</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoroolefin" title="Hydrofluoroolefin">Hydrofluoroolefin</a> (HFO) coming to prominence owing to its <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> of less than 1% that of HFC-134a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HFO_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-HFO-211">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymers_2">Polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg"><img alt="Shiny spherical drop of water on blue cloth" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="856" data-file-width="1016" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg/220px-A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg/330px-A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg/440px-A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_water_droplet_DWR-coated_surface2_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fluorosurfactant-treated fabrics are often <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fluoropolymer" title="Fluoropolymer">Fluoropolymer</a></div>
<p>About 180,000 metric tons of fluoropolymers were produced in 2006 and 2007, generating over $3.5 billion revenue per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yearly_FP_production_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-yearly_FP_production-212">[194]</a></sup> The global market was estimated at just under $6 billion in 2011 and was predicted to grow by 6.5% per year up to 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[195]</a></sup> Fluoropolymers can only be formed by <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerizing</a> free radicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlsonScmiegel20053-178">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE), sometimes called by its DuPont name Teflon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_of_structure_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyc_of_structure-214">[196]</a></sup> represents 60–80% by mass of the world's fluoropolymer production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yearly_FP_production_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-yearly_FP_production-212">[194]</a></sup> The largest application is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_insulation" title="Electrical insulation">electrical insulation</a> since PTFE is an excellent <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a>. It is also used in the chemical industry where corrosion resistance is needed, in coating pipes, tubing, and gaskets. Another major use is in PFTE-coated <a href="/wiki/Fabric_structure" title="Fabric structure">fiberglass cloth</a> for stadium roofs. The major consumer application is for <a href="/wiki/Non-stick_surface" title="Non-stick surface">non-stick cookware</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_of_structure_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-encyc_of_structure-214">[196]</a></sup> Jerked PTFE film becomes expanded PTFE (ePTFE), a fine-pored <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_membrane" title="Synthetic membrane">membrane</a> sometimes referred to by the brand name <a href="/wiki/Gore-Tex" title="Gore-Tex">Gore-Tex</a> and used for rainwear, <a href="/wiki/Personal_protective_equipment" title="Personal protective equipment">protective apparel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Filtration" title="Filtration">filters</a>; <a href="/wiki/PTFE_fiber" title="PTFE fiber">ePTFE fibers</a> may be made into <a href="/wiki/Seal_(mechanical)" title="Seal (mechanical)">seals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust_collector" title="Dust collector">dust filters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_of_structure_214-2"><a href="#cite_note-encyc_of_structure-214">[196]</a></sup> Other fluoropolymers, including <a href="/wiki/Fluorinated_ethylene_propylene" title="Fluorinated ethylene propylene">fluorinated ethylene propylene</a>, mimic PTFE's properties and can substitute for it; they are more moldable, but also more costly and have lower thermal stability. Films from two different fluoropolymers replace glass in solar cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_of_structure_214-3"><a href="#cite_note-encyc_of_structure-214">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeBergalis_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeBergalis-215">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>The chemically resistant (but expensive) fluorinated <a href="/wiki/Ionomer" title="Ionomer">ionomers</a> are used as electrochemical cell membranes, of which the first and most prominent example is <a href="/wiki/Nafion" title="Nafion">Nafion</a>. Developed in the 1960s, it was initially deployed as fuel cell material in spacecraft and then replaced mercury-based <a href="/wiki/Chloralkali_process" title="Chloralkali process">chloralkali process</a> cells. Recently, the fuel cell application has reemerged with efforts to install <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_exchange_membrane" title="Proton exchange membrane">proton exchange membrane</a> fuel cells into automobiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fluorinated_Ionomers_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fluorinated_Ionomers-216">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Functional_Materials_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Functional_Materials-217">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chlor-alkali_and_chlorate_technology_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chlor-alkali_and_chlorate_technology-218">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fluoroelastomer" title="Fluoroelastomer">Fluoroelastomers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Viton" title="Viton">Viton</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crosslink" title="Crosslink">crosslinked</a> fluoropolymer mixtures mainly used in <a href="/wiki/O-ring" title="O-ring">O-rings</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc_of_structure_214-4"><a href="#cite_note-encyc_of_structure-214">[196]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perfluorobutane" title="Perfluorobutane">perfluorobutane</a> (C<sub>4</sub>F<sub>10</sub>) is used as a fire-extinguishing agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlye201210_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlye201210-219">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surfactants">Surfactants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Surfactants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorinated_surfactant" title="Fluorinated surfactant">Fluorinated surfactant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durable_water_repellent" title="Durable water repellent">Durable water repellent</a></div>
<p>Fluorosurfactants are small organofluorine molecules used for repelling water and stains. Although expensive (comparable to pharmaceuticals at $200–2000 per kilogram), they yielded over $1 billion in annual revenues by 2006; <a href="/wiki/Scotchgard" title="Scotchgard">Scotchgard</a> alone generated over $300 million in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorosurfactant_market_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-fluorosurfactant_market-207">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fluorinated_surfactants_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fluorinated_surfactants-220">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullman_fibers_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullman_fibers-221">[203]</a></sup> Fluorosurfactants are a minority in the overall surfactant market, most of which is taken up by much cheaper hydrocarbon-based products. Applications in <a href="/wiki/Surfactants_in_paint" title="Surfactants in paint">paints</a> are burdened by <a href="/wiki/Compounding" title="Compounding">compounding</a> costs; this use was valued at only $100 million in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorosurfactant_market_207-2"><a href="#cite_note-fluorosurfactant_market-207">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agrichemicals">Agrichemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Agrichemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>About 30% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrichemical" title="Agrichemical">agrichemicals</a> contain fluorine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[204]</a></sup> most of them <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a> with a few <a href="/wiki/Plant_hormone" title="Plant hormone">crop regulators</a>. Fluorine substitution, usually of a single atom or at most a <a href="/wiki/Trifluoromethyl" title="Trifluoromethyl">trifluoromethyl</a> group, is a robust modification with effects analogous to fluorinated pharmaceuticals: increased biological stay time, membrane crossing, and altering of molecular recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodoridis_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodoridis-223">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trifluralin" title="Trifluralin">Trifluralin</a> is a prominent example, with large-scale use in the U.S. as a weedkiller,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodoridis_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theodoridis-223">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trifluralin_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-trifluralin-224">[206]</a></sup> but it is a suspected carcinogen and has been banned in many European countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[207]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_monofluoroacetate" title="Sodium monofluoroacetate">Sodium monofluoroacetate</a> (1080) is a mammalian poison in which two <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> hydrogens are replaced with fluorine and sodium; it disrupts cell metabolism by replacing acetate in the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. First synthesized in the late 19th century, it was recognized as an insecticide in the early 20th, and was later deployed in its current use. New Zealand, the largest consumer of 1080, uses it to protect <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwis</a> from the invasive Australian <a href="/wiki/Common_brushtail_possum" title="Common brushtail possum">common brushtail possum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beasley_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beasley-226">[208]</a></sup> Europe and the U.S. have banned 1080.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proudfoot_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Proudfoot-227">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[note 19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicinal_applications">Medicinal applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Medicinal applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dental_care">Dental care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Dental care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_090526-F-1333S-023_A_service_member_embarked_aboard_the_Military_Sealift_Command_hospital_ship_USNS_Comfort_(T-AH_20)_gives_a_Fluoride_treatment_to_a_patient_during_a_Continuing_Promise_2009_medical_civil_service_projec.jpg"><img alt="Man holding plastic tray with brown material in it and sticking a small stick into a boy's open mouth" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1860" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/US_Navy_090526-F-1333S-023_A_service_member_embarked_aboard_the_Military_Sealift_Command_hospital_ship_USNS_Comfort_%28T-AH_20%29_gives_a_Fluoride_treatment_to_a_patient_during_a_Continuing_Promise_2009_medical_civil_service_projec.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/US_Navy_090526-F-1333S-023_A_service_member_embarked_aboard_the_Military_Sealift_Command_hospital_ship_USNS_Comfort_%28T-AH_20%29_gives_a_Fluoride_treatment_to_a_patient_during_a_Continuing_Promise_2009_medical_civil_service_projec.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/US_Navy_090526-F-1333S-023_A_service_member_embarked_aboard_the_Military_Sealift_Command_hospital_ship_USNS_Comfort_%28T-AH_20%29_gives_a_Fluoride_treatment_to_a_patient_during_a_Continuing_Promise_2009_medical_civil_service_projec.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_090526-F-1333S-023_A_service_member_embarked_aboard_the_Military_Sealift_Command_hospital_ship_USNS_Comfort_(T-AH_20)_gives_a_Fluoride_treatment_to_a_patient_during_a_Continuing_Promise_2009_medical_civil_service_projec.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Topical fluoride treatment in Panama</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fluoride_therapy" title="Fluoride therapy">Fluoride therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" title="Water fluoridation">Water fluoridation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation_controversy" title="Water fluoridation controversy">Water fluoridation controversy</a></div>
<p>Population studies from the mid-20th century onwards show <a href="/wiki/Topical_medication" title="Topical medication">topical</a> fluoride reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_caries" title="Dental caries">dental caries</a>. This was first attributed to the conversion of tooth enamel <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a> into the more durable fluorapatite, but studies on pre-fluoridated teeth refuted this hypothesis, and current theories involve fluoride aiding enamel growth in small caries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzo_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzo-230">[211]</a></sup> After studies of children in areas where fluoride was naturally present in drinking water, controlled <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">public water supply</a> fluoridation to fight tooth decay<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[212]</a></sup> began in the 1940s and is now applied to water supplying 6 percent of the global population, including two-thirds of Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ripa_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ripa-232">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheng_Chalmers_Sheldon_2007_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheng_Chalmers_Sheldon_2007-233">[214]</a></sup> Reviews of the scholarly literature in 2000 and 2007 associated water fluoridation with a significant reduction of tooth decay in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHMRC_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHMRC-234">[215]</a></sup> Despite such endorsements and evidence of no adverse effects other than mostly benign <a href="/wiki/Dental_fluorosis" title="Dental fluorosis">dental fluorosis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation_controversy" title="Water fluoridation controversy">opposition</a> still exists on ethical and safety grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheng_Chalmers_Sheldon_2007_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cheng_Chalmers_Sheldon_2007-233">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[217]</a></sup> The benefits of fluoridation have lessened, possibly due to other fluoride sources, but are still measurable in low-income groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dental_caries_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dental_caries-237">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_monofluorophosphate" title="Sodium monofluorophosphate">Sodium monofluorophosphate</a> and sometimes sodium or <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_fluoride" title="Tin(II) fluoride">tin(II) fluoride</a> are often found in fluoride <a href="/wiki/Toothpaste" title="Toothpaste">toothpastes</a>, first introduced in the U.S. in 1955 and now ubiquitous in developed countries, alongside fluoridated mouthwashes, gels, foams, and varnishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dental_caries_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dental_caries-237">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crest_continuing_ed_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crest_continuing_ed-238">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmaceuticals">Pharmaceuticals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pharmaceuticals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prozac_pills.jpg"><img alt='Capsules with "Prozac" and "DISTA" visible' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Prozac_pills.jpg/220px-Prozac_pills.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Prozac_pills.jpg/330px-Prozac_pills.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Prozac_pills.jpg/440px-Prozac_pills.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prozac_pills.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">Fluoxetine</a> capsules</div></div></div>
<p>Twenty percent of modern pharmaceuticals contain fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_2011_178_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley_2011_178-239">[220]</a></sup> One of these, the cholesterol-reducer <a href="/wiki/Atorvastatin" title="Atorvastatin">atorvastatin</a> (Lipitor), made more revenue than any other drug until it became generic in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-url_Crain's_2011_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-url_Crain's_2011-240">[221]</a></sup> The combination asthma prescription <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seretide" title="Seretide">Seretide</a>, a top-ten revenue drug in the mid-2000s, contains two active ingredients, one of which – <a href="/wiki/Fluticasone" title="Fluticasone">fluticasone</a> – is fluorinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinson_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swinson-241">[222]</a></sup> Many drugs are fluorinated to delay inactivation and lengthen dosage periods because the carbon–fluorine bond is very stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagmann_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagmann-242">[223]</a></sup> Fluorination also increases <a href="/wiki/Lipophilicity" title="Lipophilicity">lipophilicity</a> because the bond is more hydrophobic than the <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93hydrogen_bond" title="Carbon–hydrogen bond">carbon–hydrogen bond</a>, and this often helps in cell membrane penetration and hence <a href="/wiki/Bioavailability" title="Bioavailability">bioavailability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinson_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swinson-241">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tricyclic_antidepressant" title="Tricyclic antidepressant">Tricyclics</a> and other pre-1980s <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a> had several side effects due to their non-selective interference with <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a> other than the <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> target; the fluorinated <a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">fluoxetine</a> was selective and one of the first to avoid this problem. Many current antidepressants receive this same treatment, including the <a href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a>: <a href="/wiki/Citalopram" title="Citalopram">citalopram</a>, its isomer <a href="/wiki/Escitalopram" title="Escitalopram">escitalopram</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fluvoxamine" title="Fluvoxamine">fluvoxamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[225]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quinolones" title="Quinolones">Quinolones</a> are artificial <a href="/wiki/Broad-spectrum_antibiotic" title="Broad-spectrum antibiotic">broad-spectrum antibiotics</a> that are often fluorinated to enhance their effects. These include <a href="/wiki/Ciprofloxacin" title="Ciprofloxacin">ciprofloxacin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Levofloxacin" title="Levofloxacin">levofloxacin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17342653_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17342653-247">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[229]</a></sup> Fluorine also finds use in steroids:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[230]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fludrocortisone" title="Fludrocortisone">fludrocortisone</a> is a blood pressure-raising <a href="/wiki/Mineralocorticoid" title="Mineralocorticoid">mineralocorticoid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Triamcinolone" title="Triamcinolone">triamcinolone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dexamethasone" title="Dexamethasone">dexamethasone</a> are strong <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dontburn_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-dontburn-250">[231]</a></sup> The majority of inhaled <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic" title="Anesthetic">anesthetics</a> are heavily fluorinated; the prototype <a href="/wiki/Halothane" title="Halothane">halothane</a> is much more inert and potent than its contemporaries. Later compounds such as the fluorinated <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ethers</a> <a href="/wiki/Sevoflurane" title="Sevoflurane">sevoflurane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desflurane" title="Desflurane">desflurane</a> are better than halothane and are almost insoluble in blood, allowing faster waking times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fut_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-fut-251">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anestetics_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-anestetics-252">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PET_scanning">PET scanning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: PET scanning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">Positron emission tomography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PET-MIPS-anim.gif"><img alt="Rotating transparent image of a human figure with targeted organs highlighted" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="446" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PET-MIPS-anim.gif/170px-PET-MIPS-anim.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PET-MIPS-anim.gif/255px-PET-MIPS-anim.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/PET-MIPS-anim.gif/340px-PET-MIPS-anim.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PET-MIPS-anim.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A full-body <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span> PET scan with glucose tagged with radioactive fluorine-18. The normal brain and kidneys take up enough glucose to be imaged. the A malignant tumor is seen in the upper abdomen. Radioactive fluorine is seen in urine in the bladder.</div></div></div>
<p>Fluorine-18 is often found in <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracers</a> for positron emission tomography, as its half-life of almost two hours is long enough to allow for its transport from production facilities to imaging centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18F_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-18F-253">[234]</a></sup> The most common tracer is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorodeoxyglucose" title="Fluorodeoxyglucose">fluorodeoxyglucose</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18F_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-18F-253">[234]</a></sup> which, after intravenous injection, is taken up by glucose-requiring tissues such as the brain and most malignant tumors;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[235]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">computer-assisted tomography</a> can then be used for detailed imaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxygen_carriers">Oxygen carriers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Oxygen carriers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Blood_substitute" title="Blood substitute">Blood substitute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_breathing" title="Liquid breathing">Liquid breathing</a></div>
<p>Liquid fluorocarbons can hold large volumes of oxygen or carbon dioxide, more so than blood, and have attracted attention for their possible uses in artificial blood and in liquid breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabrielet_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabrielet-256">[237]</a></sup> Because fluorocarbons do not normally mix with water, they must be mixed into emulsions (small droplets of perfluorocarbon suspended in water) to be used as blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schimmeyer_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmeyer-258">[239]</a></sup> One such product, <a href="/wiki/Oxycyte" title="Oxycyte">Oxycyte</a>, has been through initial clinical trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[240]</a></sup> These substances can aid endurance athletes and are banned from sports; one cyclist's near death in 1998 prompted an investigation into their abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[242]</a></sup> Applications of pure perfluorocarbon liquid breathing (which uses pure perfluorocarbon liquid, not a water emulsion) include assisting burn victims and premature babies with deficient lungs. Partial and complete lung filling have been considered, though only the former has had any significant tests in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-262">[243]</a></sup> An Alliance Pharmaceuticals effort reached clinical trials but was abandoned because the results were not better than normal therapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alliance_stock_drop_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alliance_stock_drop-263">[244]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biological_aspects_of_fluorine" title="Biological aspects of fluorine">Biological aspects of fluorine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gifblaar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gifblaar.jpg/220px-Gifblaar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gifblaar.jpg/330px-Gifblaar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gifblaar.jpg/440px-Gifblaar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gifblaar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gifblaar" title="Gifblaar">gifblaar</a> is one of the few organofluorine-synthesizing organisms</div></div></div>
<p>Fluorine is <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">not essential</a> for humans or other mammals; small amounts may be beneficial for bone strength, but this has not been definitively established. As there are many environmental sources of trace fluorine, the possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Fluorine_deficiency" title="Fluorine deficiency">fluorine deficiency</a> could apply only to artificial diets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gastroenterology1_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-gastroenterology1-264">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quott_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-quott-265">[246]</a></sup> Natural organofluorines have been found in microorganisms and plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribble2002_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribble2002-69">[65]</a></sup> but not animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2003_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2003-266">[247]</a></sup> The most common is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoroacetate" title="Sodium fluoroacetate">fluoroacetate</a>, which is used as a <a href="/wiki/Plant_defense_against_herbivory" title="Plant defense against herbivory">defense against herbivores</a> by at least 40 plants in Africa, Australia and Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proudfoot_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-Proudfoot-227">[209]</a></sup> Other examples include terminally fluorinated <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluoroacetone" title="Fluoroacetone">fluoroacetone</a>, and 2-fluorocitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2003_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2003-266">[247]</a></sup> An enzyme that binds fluorine to carbon – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenosyl-fluoride_synthase" title="Adenosyl-fluoride synthase">adenosyl-fluoride synthase</a> – was discovered in bacteria in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[248]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biological_aspects_of_fluorine#Hazards" title="Biological aspects of fluorine">Fluorine-related hazards</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:256px;max-width:256px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DOT_hazmat_class_6.1.svg"><img alt="A diagonal placard with warning poison" data-file-height="90" data-file-width="90" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/DOT_hazmat_class_6.1.svg/60px-DOT_hazmat_class_6.1.svg.png" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DOT_hazmat_class_5.1.svg"><img alt="A diagonal placard with warning corrosive" data-file-height="90" data-file-width="90" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/DOT_hazmat_class_5.1.svg/60px-DOT_hazmat_class_5.1.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/DOT_hazmat_class_5.1.svg/120px-DOT_hazmat_class_5.1.svg.png 2x" width="60"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg"><img alt="A diagonal placard with warning inhalant" data-file-height="90" data-file-width="90" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg/60px-DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg/90px-DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg/120px-DOT_hazmat_class_8.svg.png 2x" width="60"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:62px;max-width:62px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_(alt).svg"><img alt="A diagonal placard with warning oxidant" data-file-height="90" data-file-width="90" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg/60px-DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg/90px-DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg/120px-DOT_hazmat_class_2.3_%28alt%29.svg.png 2x" width="60"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">U.S. hazard signs for commercially transported fluorine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_data_sheet_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA_data_sheet-268">[249]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Elemental fluorine is highly toxic to living organisms. Its effects in humans start at concentrations lower than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>'s 50 ppm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[250]</a></sup> and are similar to those of chlorine:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[251]</a></sup> significant irritation of the eyes and respiratory system as well as liver and kidney damage occur above 25 ppm, which is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a> value for fluorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-271">[252]</a></sup> Eyes and noses are seriously damaged at 100 ppm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-271">[252]</a></sup> and inhalation of 1,000 ppm fluorine will cause death in minutes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[253]</a></sup> compared to 270 ppm for hydrogen cyanide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[254]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrofluoric_acid">Hydrofluoric acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Hydrofluoric acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HF_burned_hands.jpg"><img alt="left and right hands, two views, burned index fingers" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="500" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/HF_burned_hands.jpg/220px-HF_burned_hands.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/HF_burned_hands.jpg/330px-HF_burned_hands.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/HF_burned_hands.jpg/440px-HF_burned_hands.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HF_burned_hands.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrofluoric acid burns may not be evident for a day, after which calcium treatments are less effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eaton_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eaton-275">[256]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_burn" title="Chemical burn">Chemical burn</a></div>
<p>Hydrofluoric acid is a contact poison with greater hazards than many strong acids like sulfuric acid even though it is weak: it remains neutral in aqueous solution and thus penetrates tissue faster, whether through inhalation, ingestion or the skin, and at least nine U.S. workers died in such accidents from 1984 to 1994. It reacts with calcium and magnesium in the blood leading to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypocalcemia" title="Hypocalcemia">hypocalcemia</a> and possible death through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">cardiac arrhythmia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSHA_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSHA-276">[257]</a></sup> Insoluble calcium fluoride formation triggers strong pain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldfrank_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldfrank-277">[258]</a></sup> and burns larger than 160 cm<sup>2</sup> (25 in<sup>2</sup>) can cause serious systemic toxicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMTHFE_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-RMTHFE-278">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure may not be evident for eight hours for 50% HF, rising to 24 hours for lower concentrations, and a burn may initially be painless as hydrogen fluoride affects nerve function. If skin has been exposed to HF, damage can be reduced by rinsing it under a jet of water for 10–15 minutes and removing contaminated clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[260]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_gluconate" title="Calcium gluconate">Calcium gluconate</a> is often applied next, providing calcium ions to bind with fluoride; skin burns can be treated with 2.5% calcium gluconate gel or special rinsing solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2741315_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2741315-280">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17091088_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17091088-281">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15461243_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15461243-282">[263]</a></sup> Hydrofluoric acid absorption requires further medical treatment; calcium gluconate may be injected or administered intravenously. Using calcium chloride – a common laboratory reagent – in lieu of calcium gluconate is contraindicated, and may lead to severe complications. Excision or amputation of affected parts may be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMTHFE_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-RMTHFE-278">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluoride_ion">Fluoride ion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Fluoride ion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fluoride_toxicity" title="Fluoride toxicity">Fluoride toxicity</a></div>
<p>Soluble fluorides are moderately toxic: 5–10 g sodium fluoride, or 32–64 mg fluoride ions per kilogram of body mass, represents a lethal dose for adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[265]</a></sup> One-fifth of the lethal dose can cause adverse health effects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nochimson_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nochimson-285">[266]</a></sup> and chronic excess consumption may lead to <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_fluorosis" title="Skeletal fluorosis">skeletal fluorosis</a>, which affects millions in Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nochimson_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nochimson-285">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19305069_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19305069-286">[267]</a></sup> Ingested fluoride forms hydrofluoric acid in the stomach which is easily absorbed by the intestines, where it crosses cell membranes, binds with calcium and interferes with various enzymes, before urinary <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretion</a>. Exposure limits are determined by urine testing of the body's ability to clear fluoride ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nochimson_285-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nochimson-285">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, most cases of fluoride poisoning have been caused by accidental ingestion of insecticides containing inorganic fluorides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Augenstein_et_al._1991_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-Augenstein_et_al._1991-288">[269]</a></sup> Most current calls to poison control centers for possible fluoride poisoning come from the ingestion of fluoride-containing toothpaste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nochimson_285-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nochimson-285">[266]</a></sup> Malfunctioning water fluoridation equipment is another cause: one incident in Alaska affected almost 300 people and killed one person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gessner94_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gessner94-289">[270]</a></sup> Dangers from toothpaste are aggravated for small children, and the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> recommends supervising children below six brushing their teeth so that they do not swallow toothpaste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[271]</a></sup> One regional study examined a year of pre-teen fluoride poisoning reports totaling 87 cases, including one death from ingesting insecticide. Most had no symptoms, but about 30% had stomach pains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Augenstein_et_al._1991_288-1"><a href="#cite_note-Augenstein_et_al._1991-288">[269]</a></sup> A larger study across the U.S. had similar findings: 80% of cases involved children under six, and there were few serious cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[272]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_concerns">Environmental concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Environmental concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif"><img alt="Animation showing colored representation of ozone distribution by year above North America in 6 steps. It starts with a lot of ozone but by 2060 is all gone." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="932" data-file-width="1280" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/220px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/330px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif/440px-Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Future_ozone_layer_concentrations.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NASA projection of stratospheric ozone over North America without the Montreal Protocol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[273]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">Ozone depletion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a>, signed in 1987, set strict regulations on chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) and <a href="/wiki/Bromofluorocarbon" title="Bromofluorocarbon">bromofluorocarbons</a> due to their ozone damaging potential (ODP). The high stability which suited them to their original applications also meant that they were not decomposing until they reached higher altitudes, where liberated chlorine and bromine atoms attacked ozone molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_FAQ_Ozone_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP_FAQ_Ozone-293">[274]</a></sup> Even with the ban, and early indications of its efficacy, predictions warned that several generations would pass before full recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[276]</a></sup> With one-tenth the ODP of CFCs, hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HCFCs) are the current replacements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_OLP_Science_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_OLP_Science-296">[277]</a></sup> and are themselves scheduled for substitution by 2030–2040 by hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) with no chlorine and zero ODP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCFC0_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCFC0-297">[278]</a></sup> In 2007 this date was brought forward to 2020 for developed countries;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCFC_Elim_speedup_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCFC_Elim_speedup-298">[279]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> had already prohibited one HCFC's production and capped those of two others in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCFC0_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-HCFC0-297">[278]</a></sup> Fluorocarbon gases are generally <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global-warming_potential" title="Global-warming potential">global-warming potentials</a> (GWPs) of about 100 to 10,000; sulfur hexafluoride has a value of around 20,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster_et_al.2007212–213_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster_et_al.2007212–213-299">[280]</a></sup> An outlier is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HFO-1234yf" title="HFO-1234yf">HFO-1234yf</a> which is a new type of refrigerant called a <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoroolefin" title="Hydrofluoroolefin">Hydrofluoroolefin</a> (HFO) and has attracted global demand due to its GWP of 4 compared to 1,430 for the current refrigerant standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HFC-134a" title="HFC-134a">HFC-134a</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HFO_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-HFO-211">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biopersistence">Biopersistence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Biopersistence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PFOS-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1100" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/PFOS-3D-vdW.png/220px-PFOS-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/PFOS-3D-vdW.png/330px-PFOS-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/PFOS-3D-vdW.png/440px-PFOS-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PFOS-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Perfluorooctanesulfonic_acid" title="Perfluorooctanesulfonic acid">Perfluorooctanesulfonic acid</a>, a key <a href="/wiki/Scotchgard" title="Scotchgard">Scotchgard</a> component until 2000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwarcz200437_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwarcz200437-300">[281]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biological_aspects_of_fluorine#Biopersistence" title="Biological aspects of fluorine">Biopersistence of fluorinated organics</a></div>
<p>Organofluorines exhibit biopersistence due to the strength of the carbon–fluorine bond. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluoroalkyl_acid" title="Perfluoroalkyl acid">Perfluoroalkyl acids</a> (PFAAs), which are sparingly water-soluble owing to their acidic functional groups, are noted <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[282]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perfluorooctanesulfonic_acid" title="Perfluorooctanesulfonic acid">perfluorooctanesulfonic acid</a> (PFOS) and <a href="/wiki/Perfluorooctanoic_acid" title="Perfluorooctanoic acid">perfluorooctanoic acid</a> (PFOA) are most often researched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA-302">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_evidence_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA_evidence-303">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[285]</a></sup> PFAAs have been found in trace quantities worldwide from polar bears to humans, with PFOS and PFOA known to reside in breast milk and the blood of newborn babies. A 2013 review showed a slight correlation between groundwater and soil PFAA levels and human activity; there was no clear pattern of one chemical dominating, and higher amounts of PFOS were correlated to higher amounts of PFOA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_302-1"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA-302">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_evidence_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA_evidence-303">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[286]</a></sup> In the body, PFAAs bind to proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Serum_albumin" title="Serum albumin">serum albumin</a>; they tend to concentrate within humans in the liver and blood before excretion through the kidneys. Dwell time in the body varies greatly by species, with half-lives of days in rodents, and years in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_302-2"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA-302">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_evidence_303-2"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA_evidence-303">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tox_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-tox-306">[287]</a></sup> High doses of PFOS and PFOA cause cancer and death in newborn rodents but human studies have not established an effect at current exposure levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_302-3"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA-302">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFOA_evidence_303-3"><a href="#cite_note-PFOA_evidence-303">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tox_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-tox-306">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fluoride_selective_electrode" title="Fluoride selective electrode">Fluoride selective electrode</a>, which measures fluoride concentration</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fluorine_absorption_dating" title="Fluorine absorption dating">Fluorine absorption dating</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorine-19_nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Fluorine-19 nuclear magnetic resonance"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">19</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>F</span> NMR</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fluorous_chemistry" title="Fluorous chemistry">Fluorous chemistry</a>, a process used to separate reagents from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_solvents" title="Organic solvents">organic solvents</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Krypton_fluoride_laser" title="Krypton fluoride laser">Krypton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluoride_laser" title="Argon fluoride laser">argon fluoride lasers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Electrophilic_fluorination" title="Electrophilic fluorination">Electrophilic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radical_fluorination" title="Radical fluorination">radical</a> fluorination</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fluorine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources disagree on the radii of oxygen, fluorine, and neon atoms. Precise comparison is thus impossible.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">α-Fluorine has a regular pattern of molecules and is a crystalline solid, but its molecules do not have a specific orientation. β-Fluorine's molecules have fixed locations and minimal rotational uncertainty. For further detail on α-fluorine, see the 1970 structure by Pauling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> For further detail on the concept of disorder in crystals, see the referenced general reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A loud click is heard. Samples may shatter and sample windows blow out.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The ratio of the angular momentum to magnetic moment is called the gyromagnetic ratio. "Certain nuclei can for many purposes be thought of as spinning round an axis like the Earth or like a top. In general the spin endows them with angular momentum and with a magnetic moment; the first because of their mass, the second because all or part of their electric charge may be rotating with the mass."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilius_Valentinus" title="Basilius Valentinus">Basilius Valentinus</a> supposedly described fluorite in the late 15th century, but because his writings were uncovered 200 years later, this work's veracity is doubtful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopSci-74">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup></span>
