<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Or perhaps from as early as 1670 onwards; Partington<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partington-81">[76]</a></sup> and Weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-80">[75]</a></sup> give differing accounts.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fl, since 2012, is used for <a href="/wiki/Flerovium" title="Flerovium">flerovium</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Davy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Gay-Lussac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Thénard</a>, and the Irish chemists Thomas and George Knox were injured. Belgian chemist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paulin_Louyet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paulin Louyet (page does not exist)">Paulin Louyet</a> and French chemist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Nickl%C3%A8s&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jérôme Nicklès (page does not exist)">Jérôme Nicklès</a> died. Moissan also experienced serious hydrogen fluoride poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toon_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toon-91">[84]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Also honored was his invention of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fluorine in <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is defined to have oxidation state 0.  The unstable species <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, which decompose at around 40 K, have intermediate oxidation states;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001422_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWibergWibergHolleman2001422-104">[95]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and a few related species are predicted to be stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[96]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The metastable <a href="/wiki/Boron_monofluoride" title="Boron monofluoride">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_monofluoride" title="Nitrogen monofluoride">nitrogen monofluoride</a> have higher-order fluorine bonds, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_complex" title="Metal complex">metal complexes</a> use it as a <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligand</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonding" title="Hydrogen bonding">Hydrogen bonding</a> is another possibility.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZrF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> melts at 932 °C (1710 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.96_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.96-120">[109]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap">HfF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> sublimes at 968 °C (1774 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_2011_193_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perry_2011_193-117">[106]</a></sup> and <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> melts at 1036 °C (1897 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044.92_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044.92-121">[110]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These thirteen are those of molybdenum, technetium, ruthenium, rhodium, tungsten, rhenium, osmium, iridium, platinum, polonium, uranium, neptunium, and plutonium.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also the explanation by Clark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[128]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carbon tetrafluoride is formally organic, but is included here rather than in the <a href="#Organic_compounds">organofluorine chemistry section</a> – where more complex carbon-fluorine compounds are discussed – for comparison with <span class="chemf nowrap">SiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">GeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-171"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-171">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Perfluorocarbon</i> and <i>fluorocarbon</i> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> synonyms for molecules containing carbon and fluorine only, but in colloquial and commercial contexts the latter term may refer to any carbon- and fluorine-containing molecule, possibly with other elements.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-175"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-175">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This terminology is imprecise, and <i>perfluorinated substance</i> is also used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[158]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-209">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This DuPont trademark is sometimes further misused for CFCs, HFCs, or HCFCs.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-229">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">American sheep and cattle collars may use 1080 against predators like coyotes.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
